Title: Adam Converges Without Any Modification On Update Rules

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2603.02092v1

Published Time: Tue, 03 Mar 2026 04:10:48 GMT

# Adam Converges Without Any Modification On Update Rules 

## Yushun Zhang 12 , Bingran Li 1, Congliang Chen 1, Zhi-Quan Luo 12 , Ruoyu Sun 12‚Ä† 1The Chinese University of Hong Kong, Shenzhen, China 

2Shenzhen Research Institute of Big Data 

## {yushunzhang,bingranli,congliangchen}@link.cuhk.edu.cn ,

## {sunruoyu,luozq}@cuhk.edu.cn 

Abstract 

Adam is the default algorithm for training neural networks, including large language models (LLMs). However, Reddi et al. [2018] provided an example that Adam diverges, raising concerns for its deployment in AI model training. We identify a key mismatch between the divergence example and practice: Reddi et al. [2018] pick the problem after picking the hyperparameters of Adam, i.e., (ő≤1, ő≤2); while practical applications often fix the problem first and then tune (ő≤1, ő≤2). In this work, we prove that Adam converges with proper problem-dependent hyperparameters. First, we prove that Adam converges when ő≤2 is large and ő≤1 < pő≤2. Second, when ő≤2 is small, we point out a region of (ő≤1, ő≤2) combinations where Adam can diverge to infinity. Our results indicate a phase transition for Adam from divergence to convergence when changing the (ő≤1, ő≤2) combination. To our knowledge, this is the first phase transition in (ő≤1, ő≤2) 2D-plane reported in the literature, providing rigorous theoretical guarantees for Adam optimizer. We further point out that the critical boundary (ő≤‚ąó

> 1

, ő≤‚ąó 

> 2

) is problem-dependent, and particularly, dependent on batch size. This provides suggestions on how to tune ő≤1 and ő≤2: when Adam does not work well, we suggest tuning up ő≤2 inversely with batch size to surpass the threshold ő≤‚ąó

> 2

, and then trying ő≤1 < pő≤2. Our suggestions are supported by reports from several empirical studies, which observe improved LLM training performance when applying them. 

# 1 Introduction 

Machine learning tasks often aim to solve the following empirical risk minimization (ERM) problem.

fi (x), (1.1) where x ‚ąą Rd denotes the trainable parameters, n ‚ąą N is the number of mini-batches that partition the dataset, and fi (x) denotes the loss on the i-th mini-batch data. For a fixed dataset of size D, the batch size in each mini-batch is D/n. In deep learning, Adam [Kingma and Ba, 2014] is one of the most popular algorithms for solving (1.1) . It has been applied to various domains such as natural language processing (NLP) and computer vision (CV) (e.g., [Vaswani et al., 2017, Dosovitskiy et al., 2021]). Its impact is also evidenced by over 230,000 citations as of December 2025, a number that continues to grow rapidly [Scholar, 2025]. In the era of large language models (e.g., [OpenAI, 2022]), Adam plays a central role in large-scale training. Adam is reported to be used to train many mainstream LLMs, including Llama series [Touvron

et al., 2023], Qwen series [Bai et al., 2023], and DeepSeek series [Liu et al., 2024], etc. Adam is clearly serving as a major horsepower behind the advancement of AI. Its influence was recently recognized when it received the ICLR 2025 Test-of-Time Award [ICLR, 2025]. Despite its prevalence, an influential paper [Reddi et al., 2018] (the winner of ICLR 2018 Best Paper Award 

[ICLR, 2018]) provides an example that Adam diverges with a wide range of hyperparameters. A main result in [Reddi et al., 2018] states that: 

[Reddi et al., 2018]: For any ő≤1, ő≤2 s.t. 0 ‚Č§ ő≤1 < pő≤2 < 1, there exists a problem such that Adam diverges. 

Here, ő≤1 and ő≤2 are the hyperparameters to control Adam‚Äôs 1st-order and 2nd-order momentum mk and 

vk. The divergence region is visualized in Figure 1 (a). This finding raises serious concerns for Adam‚Äôs deployment in AI model training, where the divergence can raise alerts of unpredictable training failures. Since then, many new variants have been designed. For instance, AMSGrad [Reddi et al., 2018] enforces 

vk (defined later in Algorithm 1) to be non-decreasing; AdaBound [Luo et al., 2019a] imposes constraint 

vk ‚ąą [Cl , Cu] to ensure the boundedness on the effective stepsize. On the other hand, counter-intuitively, vanilla Adam remains exceptionally popular. Without any modification on its update rules, Adam works well in practice. This is rather surprising due to the existence of divergence theory. Even more mysteriously, we find that the commonly reported hyperparameters actually satisfy the divergence condition stated earlier. For instance, Kingma and Ba [2014] claim that 

(ő≤1, ő≤2) = ( 0.9, 0.999 ) is a ‚Äúgood choice for the tested machine learning problems‚ÄĚ and it is indeed the default setting in deep learning libraries. GAN researchers (e.g. Radford et al. [2015], Isola et al. [2017]) use 

(ő≤1, ő≤2) = ( 0.5, 0.999 ). For LLMs such as GPT-3, Llama series, and DeepSeek series [Brown et al., 2020, Touvron et al., 2023, Liu et al., 2024], (ő≤1, ő≤2) is chosen to be (0.9, 0.95 ). All these hyperparameters live in the divergence region ő≤1 < pő≤2. Surprisingly, instead of observing the divergence issue, these hyperparameters achieve good performances and they actually show the sign of convergence. Why does Adam work well despite its theoretical divergence issue? Is there any mismatch between deep learning problems and the divergence example? We take a closer look into the divergence example and find out the mismatch does exist. In particular, we notice an important (but often ignored) characteristic of the divergence example: [Reddi et al., 2018] picks (ő≤1, ő≤2) before picking the problem (or precisely, the # mini-batches n). That is, to construct the divergence example, they change n for different (ő≤1, ő≤2). For instance, for (ő≤1, ő≤2) = ( 0, 0.99 ), they use one n to construct the divergent example; for (ő≤1, ő≤2) = ( 0, 0.9999 ),they use another n to construct another divergent example. On the other hand, in practical applications of Adam listed above, practitioners tune hyperparameters (ő≤1, ő≤2) after the problem (or n) is fixed. So there is a 2gap between the setting of theory and practice: the order of picking the problem and (ő≤1, ő≤2) is different. Given the good practical performance of Adam, we conjecture that Adam can converge when the problem is fixed. Unfortunately, the behavior of vanilla Adam is far less studied than its variants (perhaps due to the criticism of divergence). To verify this conjecture, we run Adam for the classification problem on data MNIST and CIFAR-10 as shown in Figure 1 (c) and (d). For these two problems, we find that: First, when ő≤2

is large, the optimization error is small for almost all values of ő≤1; Second, when ő≤1, ő≤2 are both small, there is a red region with 1.4x larger error. While Adam‚Äôs performances seem unstable in the red region, we find that it always performs well in the top blue region in Figure 1. This seems to suggest that: when the problem is fixed, Adam can converge without modification after proper tuning of ő≤1 and ő≤2. We ask the following question: 

Can Adam provably converge without any modification on its update rules? 

In this work, we theoretically characterize Adam‚Äôs behavior across different choices of (ő≤1, ő≤2). We show that vanilla Adam (without algorithmic modification) exhibits two qualitatively different regimes: in a safe 

region of (ő≤1, ő≤2) it provably converges to the set of critical points (in the realizable case) or to a neighborhood of that set (in the non-realizable case), whereas in a danger region it can diverge to infinity. Together, these results reveal a divergence‚Äďconvergence phase transition in the (ő≤1, ő≤2) plane. Our contributions are visualized in Figure 1(b) and summarized as follows. ‚ÄĘ Convergence for large ő≤2. When 0 ‚Č§ ő≤1 < pő≤2 < 1 and ő≤2 is larger than a problem-dependent threshold, Adam converges to critical points in the realizable case and to a neighborhood of critical points in the non-realizable case. The threshold for ő≤2 depends on the problem class, and it increases with n (equivalently, it decreases with batch size). ‚ÄĘ Divergence for small ő≤2. For any n ‚Č• 3 and any problem-class parameters (L, D0, D1) with large enough 

> D1

‚ÄĒwhere L is the smoothness constant (Assumption 2.1) and (D0, D1) quantify the gradient variance condition (Assumption 2.2)‚ÄĒthere exists an instance in the problem class such that Adam diverges to infinity for all (ő≤1, ő≤2) in a certain region. The boundary of the divergence region expands with n (or decreases with batch size). ‚ÄĘ Key characteristics. We emphasize the following aspects of our results. 

(1) Phase transition . The divergence result considers the same setting as our convergence result (fixed problem class), indicating the existence of a critical boundary (ő≤‚ąó   

> 1,ő≤‚ąó
> 2)

that demarcates a phase transition from divergence to convergence. 

(2) Problem-dependent bounds . Our convergence and divergence regions of (ő≤1, ő≤2) depend on the problem-class parameters. This is drastically different from [Reddi et al., 2018], which considers problem-independent (ő≤1, ő≤2).

(3) Non-asymptotic characterization . Our ‚Äúdivergence region‚ÄĚ of (ő≤1, ő≤2) expands as n increases and converges to the whole region [0, 1 )2 as n goes to infinity, which recovers (actually stronger than) the problem-independent divergence result of [Reddi et al., 2018]. In this sense, we can view the divergence result of [Reddi et al., 2018] as an asymptotic characterization of the divergence region (as n ‚Üí ‚ąě) and our divergence result as a non-asymptotic characterization (for any fixed and finite n). 

(4) No assumption of bounded gradients. Our convergence analysis does not need a bounded gradient assumption, which is commonly used in the literature. Removing such assumption is crucial for revealing the phase transition: with gradients bounded a priori, the gradients of Adam cannot diverge, while we prove that it can happen under certain (ő≤1, ő≤2).‚ÄĘ The primary challenge in the convergence proof is characterizing the limit behavior of a stochastic non-linear dynamics. We handle this by considering a concentration effect of Adam‚Äôs 1 /‚ąövk around   

> 1/pE(vk)

. One specific difficulty is that vk can be arbitrarily close to 0, and 1 /‚ąövk can behave badly in the worst case. We find that a large ő≤2 helps the concentration and stabilizes the dynamic system. The intuition is that a large ő≤2 slows down the changes of vk, and its behavior becomes predictable. 3‚ÄĘ Tuning suggestions for (ő≤1, ő≤2). Our positive and negative results provide guidance for tuning ő≤1 and 

> ő≤2

: when Adam does not work well, we suggest tuning up ő≤2 inversely with batch size to surpass the threshold ő≤‚ąó

> 2

, and then trying ő≤1 < pő≤2. These suggestions are supported by empirical findings in LLM pre-training [Porian et al., 2024, Zhang et al., 2024a]. We believe our results advance the theoretical understanding of Adam. While Reddi et al. [2018] state that ‚ÄúAdam can diverge‚ÄĚ, our results show the other side of the story: when the problem is fixed, proper tuning can make Adam converge without any modification. In particular, the convergence region of (ő≤1, ő≤2)

is guaranteed to be nonempty. This is analogous to gradient descent on an L-smooth objective function, where a convergent stepsize always exists (e.g. ő∑ < 2 /L). Overall, our results provide theoretical support for vanilla Adam and offer concrete guidance for selecting (ő≤1, ő≤2) in practice. 

Relation to the conference version [Zhang et al., 2022b]. This manuscript is an extended journal version of our NeurIPS 2022 conference paper [Zhang et al., 2022b]. The current manuscript presents a streamlined convergence proof for Adam under random shuffling (greatly simplified relative to the conference version) and establishes a new convergence result and proof under with-replacement sampling, which was not covered in the conference version. Technically, this journal version develops new concentration results along Adam‚Äôs trajectory and introduces a systematic procedure to deploy these concentration tools in the presence of momentum and unbounded gradients. These changes help reduce the length of the random-shuffling proof from approximately 44 pages to about 18 pages, and they enable the with-replacement convergence analysis. These proof techniques are new relative to the conference version and may be broadly applicable to analyzing Adam-type algorithms.

(2.1) In Assumption 2.1, the Lipschitz condition for component functions is standard for ERM problems (e.g., [Bertsekas and Tsitsiklis, 1996, 2000, Bubeck, 2015, Schmidt et al., 2017, Allen-Zhu, 2018]). The ‚Äúaffine variance‚ÄĚ condition is originally proposed by [Bertsekas and Tsitsiklis, 2000] and is later popularized by [Bottou et al., 2018].

The ‚Äúbounded variance‚ÄĚ condition is widely used for analyzing both SGD and adaptive gradient methods (e.g. [Polyak and Juditsky, 1992, Ghadimi and Lan, 2013, Ghadimi et al., 2016, Zaheer et al., 2018]). Assumption 2.2 allows the variance to grow with the gradient norm, making it strictly weaker than the 5bounded variance condition (2.3) . This relaxation is meaningful: bounded variance is often too restrictive and can easily fail. For example, consider a convex quadratic minimization problem [Bottou et al., 2018]:

(2.4) where A has rank at least 2. In this case, the left-hand side of (2.3) grows quadratically with ‚ą•x‚ą•2, and no finite D0 satisfies (2.3) . In contrast, Assumption 2.2 holds with finite D1 and D0. We provide a more detailed justification in Appendix A.2. Another drawback of the bounded variance condition (2.3) is that it excludes some divergence counter-examples of SignSGD (which is equivalent to Adam with ő≤1 = ő≤2 = 0). As such, analysis under (2.3) may not reveal the full picture of Adam. We provide more detailed explanation later in Section 3.2. There is also a stronger condition that requires D1 = 0, i.e., ‚ąĎn‚ąí1     

> i=0‚ą•‚ąá fi(x)‚ą•22‚Č§D0

. This condition is sometimes called ‚Äúbounded 2nd-order moment‚ÄĚ (e.g., [Nemirovski et al., 2009]). This is strictly stronger than (2.3) and implies bounded gradient condition, so it is even more restricted. As a result, Assumption 2.2 with generic D1 is recommended in [Bottou et al., 2018] as it is ‚Äúrelatively minor‚ÄĚ and ‚Äúvariance is allowed to grow quadratically in any direction.‚ÄĚ 

Now we discuss D0. When D0 = 0, the condition becomes ‚Äústrong growth condition‚ÄĚ (SGC) [Solodov, 1998, Schmidt and Roux, 2013]. An implication of SGC is that when ‚ą•‚ąá f (x)‚ą• = 0, we have ‚ą•‚ąá fi (x)‚ą• = 0 for all i. This condition is considered reasonable in the overparameterized regime where neural networks can interpolate all data points [Vaswani et al., 2019, Shi et al., 2020]. We will show that Adam converges to exact critical points when SGC holds. For the general case where D0 > 0, Adam is not guaranteed to reach the exact critical points. Instead, it can only converge to a neighborhood of critical points [Zaheer et al., 2018, Shi et al., 2020]. This phenomenon indeed occurs for Adam in experiments, even with diminishing stepsizes (see Figure 3(b)). This behavior is in line with a classical phenomenon in stochastic gradient methods: constant-stepsize SGD is known to converge to a neighborhood of critical points whose size scales with the noise level [Luo, 1991, Bertsekas, 1997, Yan et al., 2018, Yu et al., 2019, Liu et al., 2020b]. Finally, we emphasize that we do not add the bounded gradient assumption ‚ą•‚ąá f (x)‚ą• ‚Č§ G, which is commonly used in the literature. This is crucial for revealing the phase transition: with gradients bounded a priori, the gradients of Adam cannot diverge, while we prove that this can happen under certain (ő≤1, ő≤2).

## 2.2 A Brief Review of the Counter-example in [Reddi et al., 2018] 

Before stating our theoretical results, we first restate the counter-example by [Reddi et al., 2018]. For the consistency of notation, we will restate their results under our notation. They consider the following one-dimensional convex problem: minimize 1    

> n‚ąĎn‚ąí1
> i=0fi(x)

where x ‚ąą [‚ąí1, 1 ], n ‚Č• 3:  

> fi(x) =
> (
> nx ,

for i = 0 

> ‚ąíx,

otherwise, (2.5) Note that (2.5) satisfies both Assumption 2.1 and 2.2 (with D1 = n4 + n3 ‚ąí n2 and D0 = 0), so our assumptions do not rule out this counter-example a priori. Nevertheless, problem (2.5) is a constrained problem with feasible set x ‚ąą [‚ąí1, 1 ], and the optimal solution is x‚ąó = ‚ąí1. Since this is a constrained problem, the term ‚Äúdivergence‚ÄĚ here actually means the iterates will stay in a huge region with the size of the whole feasible set. Since this is a constrained problem, the term ‚Äúdivergence‚ÄĚ here actually means that the iterates will stay on the boundary of the feasible region and are far from the optimal solution x‚ąó = ‚ąí1. Note that both cases describe algorithmic behavior that is opposite to convergence, and we do not emphasize this distinction between them. In [Reddi et al., 2018], the function (2.5) is presented as an ‚Äúonline optimization problem‚ÄĚ (not a finite-sum problem). We rewrite (2.5) in a finite-sum form so that it matches our ERM notation in (1.1) . We will use the same cyclic sampling order ( f0, f1, f2; f0, f1, f2; . . . ) as in Reddi et al. [2018]. For completeness, we restate their main claim below in our notation. 6Theorem 2.3 (Theorem 2 in [Reddi et al., 2018]) . For any fixed (ő≤1, ő≤2) satisfying ő≤1 < pő≤2, there exists a sufficiently large n, s.t., applying Adam to the function (2.5) (under cyclic sampling) converges to the sub-optimal point x = 1.

We briefly discuss the divergent condition for this Theorem. As stated in Eq. (7), Appendix B in [Reddi et al., 2018], for every fixed (ő≤1, ő≤2), they need an ‚Äú n that depends on ő≤1 and ő≤2‚ÄĚ. As such, they require different n to cause divergence on different (ő≤1, ő≤2). The considered problem class is constantly changing. For completeness, we further restate Theorem 1 in [Reddi et al., 2018]. 

Theorem 2.4 (Theorem 1 in [Reddi et al., 2018]) . For function (2.5) , when ő≤1 = 0 and ő≤2 = 1 /(n2 + 1), Adam will converge to a sub-optimal point x = 1.

This theorem considers choosing (ő≤1, ő≤2) after n. However, this result only shows divergence on a single hyper-parameter choice (ő≤1, ő≤2) = ( 0, 1 /(n2 + 1)) . This configuration lies somewhere on the left boundary of Figure 1 (a). It therefore provides no characterization beyond this single point: it remains unclear which directions of changing (ő≤1, ő≤2) preserve divergence and which directions suppress it or induce convergence.   

> (a) Cyclic update order (b) Randomized update order (c) Diminishing stepsize

Figure 2: (a, b): We restate Figure 1 from [Reddi et al., 2018]. The divergence of Adam happens under both cyclic and update orders, so randomization cannot prevent divergence. Since they consider constrained problems, the term ‚Äúdivergence‚ÄĚ here means getting stuck at the sub-optimal solution x = 1. In the figures, AMSGrad is a different method, and it is not our focus. (c): Diminishing stepsize ő∑k = 1‚ąök does not prevent divergence. 

Finally, these divergence results also hold when randomized update orders are used instead of cyclic orders, as proved in [Reddi et al., 2018, Theorem 3]. Consequently, randomization does not prevent divergence. Further, the proof of [Reddi et al., 2018] uses ő∑k ‚ąĚ 1‚ąök , so diminishing stepsize does not prevent divergence, either. These claims are supported by numerical evidence in Figure 2. Reddi et al. [2018] wrote: ‚ÄúThese results have important consequences insofar as one has to use problem-dependent 

> ŌĶ

, ő≤1, and ő≤2 in order to avoid bad convergence behavior. In high-dimensional problems, this typically amounts to using...a different ŌĶ, ő≤1, and ő≤2 for each dimension. However, this defeats the purpose of adaptive methods since it requires tuning a large set of parameters.‚ÄĚ In the following, we show that in our setting such divergence can be avoided without changing the algorithm or introducing new hyperparameters. The key is to choose 

(ő≤1, ő≤2) in a problem-dependent manner, with a particular dependence on the number of mini-batches n

(equivalently, the batch size). Importantly, this does not require per-coordinate tuning of (ő≤1, ő≤2)‚ÄĒa direction mentioned in the discussion of Reddi et al. [2018]. 

## 2.3 Related Works 

Ever since Reddi et al. [2018] pointed out the divergence issue, there are many attempts on designing new variants of Adam. Since we focus on understanding vanilla Adam without modification , we do not discuss these variants here. We relegate the introduction to more Adam variants later in Appendix A.1. Compared with proposing new variants, the convergence of vanilla Adam is far less studied than its variants (perhaps due to the criticism of divergence). We discuss as follows. 7Shi et al. [2020] study randomly-shuffled RMSprop proposed in [Hinton et al., 2012] (Algorithm 2 with  

> ő≤1

= 0) 2. We believe it is important to study Adam rather than RMSProp: numerically, Adam often substantially outperforms RMSProp (e.g. [Agarwal et al., 2020]). Furthermore, all mainstream LLMs are trained using Adam, and RMSProp is reported to perform ‚Č• 20% worse than Adam [Orvieto and Gower, 2025, Table 1]. Theoretically, literature on RMSProp cannot reveal how these hyperparameters jointly affect (or jeopardize) the convergence of Adam in the (ő≤1, ő≤2)-2D plane. We note that Shi et al. [2020] conjecture the existence of a critical threshold ő≤‚ąó 

> 2

for RMSProp. We generalize this conjecture to (ő≤1, ő≤2)-2D plane for Adam and confirm the existence of a critical boundary (ő≤‚ąó  

> 1,ő≤‚ąó
> 2

). One additional difference is that Shi et al. [2020] only study randomly-shuffled RMSprop, whereas we consider more general cases of both with-replacement and randomly-shuffled Adam. D√©fossez et al. [2022] analyze Adam with 0 < ő≤2 < 1 and ő≤1 < ő≤2. However, they require bounded gradient assumption ‚ą•‚ąá f (x)‚ą• ‚Č§ G, which prevents the potential divergence a priori. Further, their bound is proportional to 1 /ŌĶ, where ŌĶ is the stability hyperparameter and is usually small (e.g., default ŌĶ is 10 ‚ąí8). As a result, they require sufficiently large non-zero ŌĶ to keep the result non-vacuous. These assumptions incur artificial boundedness property on Adam: 0 < ŌĶ ‚Č§ ‚ąöv + ŌĶ ‚Č§ G, and thus Adam will never diverge and is essentially changed to another algorithm called AdaBound [Luo et al., 2019b]. In contrast, we do not impose these boundedness conditions and reveal the di-convergence phase transition under different (ő≤1, ő≤2).

New proofs of Adam after the online appearance of the conference version in 2022. After the conference version of this manuscript [Zhang et al., 2022b] appeared online in August 2022, there has been a series of works providing new convergence proofs of Adam. Here, we summarize how their assumptions differ from ours. Recent works [Xie et al., 2024, Li et al., 2025a,b] provide new proofs for RMSProp and Adam. Compared to our work, their proof relies on a ‚Äúcoordinate-wise bounded variance‚ÄĚ condition, which imposes the inequality of (2.3) on each coordinate individually. Note that this is stronger than (2.3) , which only imposes the inequality on the overall gradient. Moreover, since (2.3) is already known to be restrictive‚ÄĒ for instance, it fails even on simple quadratic problems (2.4) ‚ÄĒits coordinate-wise variant is necessarily more limiting. Besides the coordinate-wise bounded variance, they also required T-dependent (ő≤1, ő≤2), where T is the pre-determined total iteration budget. In practice, T-dependent (ő≤1, ő≤2) is rarely used or reported by practitioners. In theory, we argue that such dependence on T is unnecessary and can be removed. In contrast, our proof does not require these two conditions. Wang et al. [2023] provide a novel convergence proof for Adam under the ‚Äúcoordinate-wise affine variance‚ÄĚ condition and T-dependent (ő≤1, ő≤2). Similarly, the coordinate-wise version is strictly stronger than the affine variance condition (2.2) or Assumption 2.2; and the T-dependence is unnecessary. Wang et al. [2024] further relax these two conditions, but their analysis focused on Adam under random shuffling and does not cover with-replacement sampling. Li et al. [2023], Peng et al. [2025], Hong and Lin [2023, 2024], Zhang et al. [2024b] provide refined convergence proofs of Adam. One limitation is that their complexity is proportional to 1 /ŌĶ, where ŌĶ is the stability hyperparameter. These bounds become vacuous as ŌĶ approaches 0, which brings concerns since  

> ŌĶ

is usually small in practice (e.g., default ŌĶ is 10 ‚ąí8). We argue that the dependency on ŌĶ is artificial, and in contrast, our result is independent of ŌĶ and allows arbitrarily small ŌĶ including 0. In addition to the dependency on ŌĶ, Li et al. [2023], Peng et al. [2025] require the bounded variance condition (2.3) or its stronger version; and Hong and Lin [2023, 2024], Zhang et al. [2024b] require an almost-sure or coordinate-wise version of the affine variance condition (2.2). All these assumptions are stronger than Assumption 2.2. Another line of new works prove the convergence of Adam under bounded gradient or iterate, or bounded vk assumptions [Ding et al., 2023, Jiang et al., 2023b, Xiao et al., 2024, Liang et al., 2025], while we do not impose these boundedness conditions. Besides the difference in assumptions, we further highlight an important difference between our work and the literature above. The above works focus on proving convergence upper bounds for Adam under      

> 2We notice that Shi et al. [2020] also analyze randomly-shuffled Adam with ő≤1close enough to 0. However, Zhang et al. [2022a] show that they require ő≤1<10 ‚ąí7. Thus, their result does not provide much extra information other than randomly-shuffled RMSProp.

8certain choices of (ő≤1, ő≤2). However, a good upper bound is just one side of the story. In contrast, we establish the phase transition from divergence to convergence in different regions of (ő≤1, ő≤2), which presents a more complete picture. Our work points out two missing facts in the literature. ‚ÄĘ First , we show that tuning (ő≤1, ő≤2) is not only sufficient but also necessary for convergence. Specifically, we prove the existence of a phase transition: as ő≤2 increases from 0 to 1, the behavior of Adam shifts from divergence (a bad lower bound in Theorem 3.5) to convergence (good upper bounds in Theorem 3.1 and 3.3). This reveals that tuning ő≤2 is not merely about optimizing the convergence rate; it is crucial for preventing the fundamental divergence danger. To our knowledge, this is the first phase transition in (ő≤1, ő≤2) 2D-plane for Adam reported in the literature. ‚ÄĘ Second , we show that this phase transition occurs at a problem-dependent boundary (ő≤‚ąó

1, ő≤‚ąó

2 ), which grows inversely with batch size . This contrasts with prior theoretical works that suggest ő≤2 should increase with the total iteration budget T and does not reveal the dependency on the considered problem.

Remark 1: the choice of ő≤2. Our theory suggests that large ő≤2 should be used to ensure convergence. This message matches our experiments in Figure 1. We emphasize that the requirement of ‚Äúlarge ő≤2‚ÄĚ is necessary, because small ő≤2 will indeed lead to provable divergence (shown later in Theorem 3.5). Note that our threshold of ő≤2 is a sufficient condition for convergence and the power is not claimed tight. Tightening the threshold for ő≤2 will be an interesting future direction. 

Remark 2: ő≤2 and batch size. We find that the condition of ő≤2: ő≤2 ‚Č• ő≥1(n) = 1 ‚ąí O

> n

(which is equivalent to larger n) requires a larger ő≤2 to reach small loss. 

Remark 3: the choice of ő≤1. Theorem 3.1 requires ő≤1 < pő≤2. Since ő≤2 is suggested to be large, our convergence result can cover flexible choice of ő≤1 ‚ąą [0, 1 ). For instance, ő≤2 = 0.999 brings the threshold of  

> ő≤1

< 0.9995 , which covers basically all practical choices of ő≤1 reported in the literature, including the default setting ő≤1 = 0.9 . Our theory aligns with our grid-search experiments on MNIST and CIFAR-10 (Figure 1 (c,d) in Section 1), where Adam shows good performance for a wide range of ő≤1 when ő≤2 is large. 

Remark 4: guidance to LLM pre-training. Our theory indicates that larger ő≤2 is required when n is large. This equivalently indicates that a larger ő≤2 is required when the batch size, which equals D 

> n

, is small. Our subsequent divergence theory (presented later) further implies that making ő≤2 dependent on the batch size is necessary to avoid divergence. Collectively, these messages can provide guidance for hyperparameter tuning in LLM pre-training, as confirmed by various literature. We list some numerical evidence as follows. ‚ÄĘ Zhao et al. [2024], Orvieto and Gower [2025] report that training fails if both (ő≤1, ő≤2) are close to 0, and the performance substantially improves when use ő≤1 = ő≤2 = 0.95 or 0.975. This finding aligns with our results. We restate some of their numerical results in Figure 4 (a). ‚ÄĘ Sre¬ī ckovi¬ī c et al. [2025] state that ‚ÄúZhang et al. [2022b] shows that higher ő≤2 values substantially improve small-batch training, and Marek et al. [2025] highlights the importance of scaling ő≤2 in this regime.‚ÄĚ We restate some of their numerical results in Figure 4 (b) and (c). ‚ÄĘ Zhang et al. [2024a] emphasize that ‚Äúlarger ő≤2 (increase from 0.95 to 0.99, 0.999, or 0.9995) substantially improves small batch size training ... aligning with findings in [Zhang et al., 2022b]‚ÄĚ . We restate some of their numerical results in Figure 4 (d, e). ‚ÄĘ Porian et al. [2024] report that ‚Äúenlarging ő≤2 (from 0.95 to 0.99 and 0.999) is essential at lower batch sizes ... This matches the theoretical work [Zhang et al., 2022b].‚ÄĚ 

Remark 5: convergence to a neighborhood of critical points. When D0 > 0, Adam converges to a neighborhood of critical points, in lieu of the exact critical points. We emphasize that this is not due to the limitation of the analysis, and this phenomenon is also observed in practice: even for simple convex quadratic function with D0 > 0, Adam with diminishing stepsize cannot reach exactly zero gradient (see Figure 3 (b). We state the non-realizable function in Appendix F). In the non-realizable case ( D0 > 0), converging to the neighborhood of critical points is common for stochastic gradient methods, including constant-stepsize SGD [Luo, 1991, Bertsekas, 1997, Yan et al., 2018, Yu et al., 2019, Liu et al., 2020b] and diminishing-stepsize RMSProp [Zaheer et al., 2018, Shi et al., 2020]. This is because: even though ő∑k is decreasing, the effective stepsize ő∑k /‚ąövk might not decay. The good news is that the size of the neighborhood O(őī(ő≤2)‚ąöD0) vanishes to 0 as ő≤2 goes to 1 (both in theory and experiments in Figure 3 (b)). This can be seen in the expression of őī(ő≤2) in (6.46) in Appendix 6. The size shrinks to 0 because the movement of ‚ąövk shrinks as ő≤2 increases.

Finally, we emphasize that the orange region in Figure 5 (a) is not discussed in [Reddi et al., 2018] because we consider n fixed while they allow n changing. With Theorem 3.1 and Theorem 3.5, we establish a clearer image on the relation between (ő≤1, ő≤2) and Adam‚Äôs qualitative behavior. 

Remark 7: on the divergence of SignSGD. Theorem 3.5 implies that SignSGD (Adam with ő≤1 = ő≤2 = 0)can diverge. The divergence can be avoided by modifying the algorithm, such as adding momentum [Sun et al., 2023, Jiang et al., 2025]; or by introducing stronger assumptions like the bounded variance condition in (2.3) and increasing batch size [Bernstein et al., 2018]. In fact, the bounded variance assumption alone is sufficient to exclude our counter-example from the considered function class, and SignSGD will converge to the neighborhood of critical points with ő∑k = ő∑0/‚ąök in that case 4. However, the analysis under bounded variance does not reveal the true divergent behavior of SignSGD. Our counter-example, which satisfies Assumption 2.2 (with D1 ‚Č• 2n2) but not bounded variance (2.3) (with D1 = n), provides additional motivation to relax (2.3) . An intriguing open question is whether there exist more counter-examples with D1 ‚ąą (n, 2 n2).We leave it for future investigation. 

Remark 8: more fine-grained characterization on the critical boundary. Integrating the divergence and convergence theories discussed above, one can conclude that there exists (at least) one critical boundary 

(ő≤‚ąó

> 1

, ő≤‚ąó 

> 2

) that demarcates the di-convergence phase transition. Since both the divergence region and conver-gence region depend on n (or equivalently, depend on batch size D 

> n

), the critical boundary (ő≤‚ąó

> 1

, ő≤‚ąó 

> 2

) must also be dependent on the batch size and is located in the white region in Fig. 1 (b). Note that our theory implies the existence of the critical boundary, whereas we have not fully determined the precise number and the shape of the boundar(-ies). Nevertheless, our experimental results in Fig. 1 (c, d) suggest that there exists only one boundary, which likely resembles the geometry of the blue region in Fig. 1 (b). We only point out its existence here and leave a more precise characterization as a future direction. 

> 4

We have not recognized a clean convergence proof of SignSGD under bounded variance (2.3) without increasing batch size in the literature, but it is expected to be a simple proof. The convergence fails when relaxing (2.3) to our Assumption 2.2. 

13 4 Key Lemmas for the Convergence Result in Theorem 3.1 

Here, we summarize the key challenges in the proof of Theorem 3.1. The proof of Theorem 3.1 can be seen in Section 7. The proof of Theorem 3.3 follows a similar idea and we relegate the proof to Appendix D. 

Additional notations. For a ‚ąą R, we use ‚Ćąa‚ĆČ to denote taking the ceiling (rounding a up). For a ‚ąą N,we denote [a] as the index set {1, ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ , a}. For the function indices n in (1.1) , we abuse the notation [n] = 

{0, ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ , n ‚ąí 1}. We denote ‚ąāl f (x) and ‚ąāl fi (x) as the l-th component of ‚ąá f (x) and ‚ąá fi (x), respectively. We use 

xl,k to denote the l-th component of x at the k-th iteration, i.e., xk. Similarly for vl,k and ml,k. We use I(¬∑) as the indicator function. E(¬∑) means taking expectation over the whole trajectory. For k ‚Č• 1, Ek [¬∑] denotes the conditional expectation given the entire history up to the end of iteration k ‚ąí 1 (i.e., given x1, m0, v0 and all past sampled indices ŌĄ1, . . . , ŌĄk‚ąí1), but excluding the fresh sample ŌĄk. We abuse the notation of őĪ as follows: for ‚ąāl főĪ(x), we define őĪ := arg max i |‚ąāl fi (x)|; for ‚ąāőĪ f (x), we define őĪ := arg max l |‚ąāl f (x)|. Similarly for mőĪ,k and 

vőĪ,k. We use ‚Äúi.i.d.‚ÄĚ and ‚Äúr.v.‚ÄĚ as abbreviations for ‚Äúindependent and identically distributed‚ÄĚ and ‚Äúrandom variable‚ÄĚ, respectively. The core of the proof is to characterize when the expected update direction of Adam E

 mk‚ąövk



constitutes a descent direction, i.e., a direction that lies in the dual cone of gradient ‚ąá f (xk ). There are at least twofold challenges. First , vk is a random variable and it appears in the denominator. This makes the entire system a stochastic non-linear dynamic system, which is difficult to analyze in general. Further, vk can potentially hit 0, which imposes extra difficulties. Second , mk contains heavy historical signals, which distort the trajectory from the gradient direction. 

Figure 7: An illustration of the changes of Adam‚Äôs update direction when ő≤2 changes. 

Our key insights are illustrated in Figure 7: we find that Adam‚Äôs update direction E

 mk‚ąövk



is close to 

‚ąá f (xk ) when ő≤2 is large (leading to convergence) and starts to deviate to the opposite direction when ő≤2

is small (causing divergence). We now introduce the proof for our convergence results. The proof for the divergence result is shown later in Section 3.2. 

Step I: Concentration effects of 1‚ąövk when ő≤2 is large. We find that 1‚ąövk concentrates around 1‚ąöEk (vk ) when 

ő≤2 is large. In particular, we prove that: 

1

‚ąövk

‚Čą 1

pEk (vk ) , w.h.p., when ő≤2 is large With this concentration property, 1‚ąövk is stabilized. Why does large ő≤2 help? Intuitively, this is because large 

ő≤2 slows down the changes of vk, and its behavior will become largely predictable. As a result, ‚ąí ‚ąá fŌĄk (xk )

> ‚ąövk

Ek

pEk (vk ) ,

14 which lies in the dual cone of the gradient direction ‚ąá f (xk ).It requires substantial effort to compare 1‚ąövk and 1‚ąöEk (vk ) . In statistical theory, it is common to show the concentration of a random variable x around its mean E(x), yet it is much less common to characterize how  

> 1‚ąöx

deviates from 1‚ąöE(x) , especially when x can be arbitrarily close to 0 and no extra boundedness condition is imposed. Without any specific property on r.v. x, every small deviation in ‚ąöx will be amplified in 1‚ąöx and it can behave very badly near zero and become arbitrarily far away from 1‚ąöE(x) [Durrett, 2019]. In Lemma 4.1, we prove that 1‚ąövk concentrates around 1‚ąöEk (vk ) when ő≤2 is large , and the result holds without 

any boundedness condition on stochastic gradients. We prove this result by utilizing two special properties of Adam: 1) stochastic gradients have a ‚Äúgeometric sum‚ÄĚ structure in vk. 2) the index of stochastic gradients are uniformly sampled from a finite index set. With these two special properties, we find the behavior of 1‚ąövk

is largely predictable when ő≤2 is large. Technically, the concentration result is established via two steps: (i) map the dynamics of 1 /‚ąövk to the dynamics of a sequence of i.i.d. Bernoulli r.v.s, which is bounded and is much easier to analyze; (ii) map the dynamics back by several decoupling steps. Step (i) and (ii) allow us to track the dynamics of possibly unbounded r.v. sequence using bounded Bernoulli proxies. The rigorous concentration effect is shown in Lemma 4.1 as follows. Lemma 4.1 can be used as a generic tool for analyzing Adam-type algorithms.

# 8 Proof of the Divergence Results in Theorem 3.5 

We now present the proof for the divergence result. We will primarily consider a = 1, which keeps the results clean. Nevertheless, the same proof procedure applies to any finite positive a > 0. We denote xk,i as the value of x at the k-th outer loop and i-th inner loop. We consider cyclic update ordering where fi (x) are sampled in the order of f0(x), f1(x), ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ , fn‚ąí1(x) within the k-th outer loop. We only present the proof for Adam under cyclic update ordering, which helps reveal the key insights. The proof for random sampling follows the same procedure and gives the similar conclusion, which we omit for brevity. Firstly, we prove the following claim: 

Claim: for any n ‚Č• 3, there exists an orange region shown in Figure 5 (a) s.t., Adam with any (ő≤1, ő≤2)

combination in the orange region gives xk+1,0 > 1 as long as xk,0 = 1.33 Now let us prove the claim. For function (3.1), the update rule of Adam is shown as follows.

It is not hard to see that ő≥1(n) is always larger than the upper boundary of the orange region, so there is no contradiction. 

# 9 Limitations and Future Directions 

‚ÄĘ More fine-grained characterization on the critical boundary. Integrating our divergence and conver-gence theories, one can conclude that there exists (at least) one critical boundary (ő≤‚ąó   

> 1,ő≤‚ąó
> 2)

that demarcates the di-convergence phase transition. However, we have not fully determined the precise number and the shape of the boundar(-ies). Our experimental results in Fig. 1 (c, d) suggest that there exists only one boundary, which likely resembles the shape of the blue region in Fig. 1 (b). We only point out its existence here and leave a more precise characterization as a future direction. ‚ÄĘ Adam v.s. SGD. In this work, we focus on the fundamental issue of convergence. One intriguing question is to verify the advantage of Adam over SGD. Identifying when and why Adam converges faster serves as an independent research topic, and we leave as future investigation. ‚ÄĘ Generalized Lipschitz conditions. A recent line of theoretical work relaxes the standard Lipschitz condition in Assumption 2.1 to some generalized Lipschitz conditions (e.g., [Zhang et al., 2019, Li et al., 2023, Wang et al., 2024]). As mentioned earlier in Section 2.1, the generalized Lipschitz conditions primarily help refine the quantitative analysis of Adam, e.g., yielding a sharper convergence rate. Meanwhile, the core focus of this work is a more basic topic: characterizing the qualitative behavior of Adam‚ÄĒsuch as phase transition from divergence to convergence. For this purpose, the standard Lipschitz condition is already adequate. Nevertheless, generalizing the Lipschitz condition is a theoretically intriguing direction for future research. ‚ÄĘ Tighter bounds. Finally, we note that in our convergence upper bounds, the O  

> log T
> ‚ąöT
> 

term has multiplicative constants of order O(d4n4.5 ), where d is the problem dimension d and n is the number of mini-batches. Similarly, the threshold of ő≤2 is in the order of n5 and n5.5 . These powers are not claimed tight, and it is possible to reduce these dependencies via a more fine-grained analysis. We leave it as an interesting future direction. ‚ÄĘ Optimal combination of ő≤1 and ő≤2. Our results point out the existence of a safe region of (ő≤1, ő≤2), but we have not yet identified which combination of ő≤1 and ő≤2 yields the optimal performance within the safe region. Recent works [Zhao et al., 2024, Orvieto and Gower, 2025] report that ő≤1 = ő≤2 (when both are large enough) usually brings optimal performance, and they provide an initial explanation. This finding is further investigated in recent works [Fern√°ndez-Hern√°ndez et al., 2026, Cattaneo and Shigida, 2026]. Identifying the optimal hyperparameter is an independent topic that requires substantially more effort. We leave it as an important future direction. 

# 10 Conclusions 

In this work, we explore the convergence of Adam. When ő≤2 is large, we prove that Adam converges with any ő≤1 < pő≤2. When ő≤2 is small, we further show that Adam can diverge to infinity for a wide range of 

> ő≤1

. The critical boundary of the phase transition (ő≤‚ąó   

> 1,ő≤‚ąó
> 2)

is problem-dependent, and in particular, depends on batch size. Our results provide rigorous theoretical groundings for Adam optimizer. These results also provide practical suggestions on how to tune ő≤1 and ő≤2.36 Acknowledgment

43 A More Discussions 

## A.1 More Related Works 

On the Variants of Adam. Ever since Reddi et al. [2018] pointed out the divergence issue of Adam, one active line of work has tried to design new variants of Adam that can be proved to converge. For instance, Zou et al. [2019], Gadat and Gavra [2020], Chen et al. [2018b, 2021] replace the constant hyperparameters by iterate-dependent ones e.g. ő≤1k or ő≤2k. AMSGrad [Reddi et al., 2018] and AdaFom [Chen et al., 2018b] modify {vk } to be a non-decreasing sequence. Iiduka [2022] further analyze the convergence of AMSGrad by relaxing the Lipschitz-gradient condition. However, their analysis requires extra conditions on both bounded gradient and bounded domain. AdaBound [Luo et al., 2019a] imposes lower and upper bounds on {vk } to prevent the effective stepsize from vanishing or exploding. Zhou et al. [2018b] also adopt a new estimate of  

> vk

to correct the bias. There are also attempts to combine Adam with Nesterov momentum [Dozat, 2016] as well as warm-up techniques [Liu et al., 2020a]. Padam [Chen et al., 2018a] also introduce a partial adaptive parameter to improve the generalization performance. There are also some works providing theoretical analysis on the variants of Adam. For instance, Zhou et al. [2018a] study the convergence of AdaGrad and AMSGrad under bounded gradient condition. Gadat and Gavra [2020] study the asymptotic behavior of a subclass of adaptive gradient methods from landscape point of view. Their analysis applies to a sub-class of Adam variants with ő≤1 = 0 and ő≤2 increasing along the iterates (it could also be understood as RMSProp with increasing ő≤2). Alacaoglu et al. [2020] study AMSGrad and two other variants of Adam. Their analysis requires both bounded gradient and bounded domain assumptions, and does not cover the original Adam. Iiduka [2022] analyze the convergence of AMSGrad by relaxing the Lipschitz-gradient condition. However, their analysis requires extra conditions on both bounded gradient and bounded domain. Another line of theoretical work imposes extra conditions on Adam‚Äôs trajectory, which implicitly modifies the update rules of Adam. De et al. [2018] analyze RMSProp and Adam, but they assume the sign of all stochastic gradients to keep the same. This requires additional projection steps in every iteration, and the resulting algorithm is no longer vanilla Adam. Barakat and Bianchi [2021] provide two proofs for Adam. The first proof considers (ő≤1, ő≤2) close to (1, 1 ) and it requires bounded gradient and bounded iterates assumptions. As discussed in the paper, we need ‚Äúa projection step on a compact set ... to ensure the boundedness of the estimates‚ÄĚ . This projection step changes the update rules of Adam, and more importantly, it eliminates the possibility of divergence a priori, and thus did not fully capture the behaviors of vanilla Adam. The second proof also requires bounded gradient assumption and it replaces the constant hyperparameters by iterate-dependent ones ő≤1k or ő≤2k under certain rules. This also changes the update rules of Adam. Huang et al. [2021] and Guo et al. [2021] propose novel and simple frameworks to analyze Adam-family with large 

> ő≤1

. Yet, they require the effective stepsize of Adam to be bounded in certain interval, i.e., 1‚ąövk +ŌĶ ‚ąą [Cl , Cu]. This boundedness condition is also imposed for RMSProp in [Zaheer et al., 2018]. This boundedness condition changes Adam into AdaBound [Luo et al., 2019a], and thus they cannot explain the divergence-convergence phase transition on the original Adam. To summarize, these works study the modified versions of Adam or implicitly impose extra operations on Adam (such as projection), which changes the update rules of Adam. Additionally, they all (including those for new variants) require bounded gradient assumptions, which prevents the potential divergence a priori. In contrast, we do not require such assumptions and prove the existence of divergence-convergence phase transition when changing (ő≤1, ő≤2).

Understanding Adam under specialized settings. There is a line of work on understanding Adam in specialized settings such as quadratic functions (e.g., [Da Silva and Gazeau, 2020, Das et al., 2024, Zhang et al., 2024c]), strongly convex functions [Dereich et al., 2025a], and deterministic and locally strongly convex functions [Dereich et al., 2025c]. Jiang et al. [2023a] study the evolution of a newly defined condition number along Adam‚Äôs trajectory on large-batch two-layer linear network. There are also theoretical works analyzing the dynamics of Adam under continuous-time approximation via ODE or SDE methods (e.g., [Da Silva and Gazeau, 2020, Malladi et al., 2022, Dereich and Jentzen, 2024, Kunstner et al., 2024, Cohen et al., 2024, Dereich 44 et al., 2025b, Li et al., 2025c]). Different from these works, we do not adopt the continuous approximation and analyze the original (and thus discrete) Adam on generic non-convex functions. There is also excellent work on understanding the benefit of AdamW over Adam under deterministic (full-batch) settings [Xie and Li, 2024], which serves as an orthogonal research topic to this work.

# B Some More Notations and Useful Lemmas 

Some More Notations. For Adam under random shuffling (Algorithm 2), we denote xk,i, mk,i, vk,i ‚ąą Rd as the value of x, m, v in the k-th outer loop and i-th inner loop. Further, we denote xl,k,i, ml,k,i, vl,k,i ‚ąą R as the 

l-th component of xk,i, mk,i, vk,i. Further, we will use ŌĄk,i to index the i-th randomly chosen batch in the k-th epoch. In this sense, we denote ‚ąāl fŌĄk,i (x) as ‚ąā‚ąāxl fŌĄk,i (x). For each epoch k ‚Č• 1, we use Ek [¬∑] as a shorthand for the conditional expectation given the history up to (and including) the epoch-start iterate xk,0 , but excluding the permutation {ŌĄk,0 , . . . , ŌĄk,n‚ąí1} sampled in epoch k. In other words, the expectation is conditioned on the initial states ( m1, ‚ąí1, v1, ‚ąí1, and x1,0 ), and all past permutations {ŌĄs,0 , . . . , ŌĄs,n‚ąí1} for s = 1, . . . , k ‚ąí 1. Similarly, Ek‚ąí1[¬∑]

denotes the conditional expectation given the entire history up to (and including) xk‚ąí1,0 , but excluding the permutation in epoch k ‚ąí 1. We abuse the notation of őĪ as follows: for ‚ąāl főĪ(x), we define őĪ := arg max i |‚ąāl fi (x)|;for ‚ąāőĪ f (x), we define őĪ := arg max l |‚ąāl f (x)|. Similarly for mőĪ,k,i and vőĪ,k,i.We further define the following constants, which will be repeatedly used in the analysis of Adam under both with-replacement sampling (Algorithm 1) and random shuffling (Algorithm 2).

Finally, when D1 = 0, i.e., when the bounded 2nd-order moment condition holds, we can arrive at a similar conclusion subject to some changes in the constant terms. The proof under D1 = 0 is strictly simpler than the current proof, as it reduces to the bounded gradient case. We conclude the proof for Theorem 3.3. 

# E Discussion on Bias Correction Terms and Non-Zero ŌĶ

In the above analysis, we focus on Adam without bias correction terms and consider ŌĶ = 0 (ŌĶ is the hyperparameter for numerical stability in Algorithm 1 and 2). For completeness, we now briefly discuss how to incorporate the bias correction terms and non-zero ŌĶ into our analysis above. Based on the current convergence proof, we only require several additional simple changes. We will first discuss non-zero ŌĶ and then discuss bias correction terms. We will use the notations for Algorithm 1 but all the arguments also hold for Algorithm 2. 

Adam with non-zero ŌĶ: In our current analysis, we consider ŌĶ = 0. In practice, ŌĶ is often set to be a small positive number such as 10 ‚ąí8. Proving convergence with ŌĶ > 0 is strictly simpler. We minimize function (3.1) using Algorithm 2 with cyclic order f0, f1, f2 and so on. We report the optimality gap x ‚ąí x‚ąó after 50k iterations, or equivalently 

50000 /n epochs. We use ŌĶ = 10 ‚ąí8 for numerical stability. We use diminishing stepsize ő∑k = 0.1 /‚ąök,where k is the index of epoch. Unless otherwise stated, this setting applies to all the other experiments on function (3.1). ‚ÄĘ MNIST [Deng, 2012]. We use one-hidden-layer neural network with width =16. We set batch size =1, weight decay =0, stepsize =0.0001 and train for 20 epochs. We use ŌĶ = 10 ‚ąí8 for numerical stability. ‚ÄĘ CIFAR-10 [Krizhevsky et al., 2009]. We use ResNet-18 [He et al., 2016] as the architecture. We choose batch size =16, weight decay =5e-4 , initial stepsize=1e-3. We use a stage-wise constant learning rate scheduling with a multiplicative factor of 0.1 on epoch 30, 60 and 90. We use ŌĶ = 10 ‚ąí8 for numerical stability.
